⚠ [493/631] Built Game.Levels.ImageWorld.L18_NonemptinessTransfer (1.3s)
warning: Game/Levels/ImageWorld/L18_NonemptinessTransfer.lean:6:0: No translation (en) found for: Image Preserves Nonemptiness
warning: Game/Levels/ImageWorld/L18_NonemptinessTransfer.lean:10:0: No translation (en) found for: # Nonemptiness Transfers Through Images

If a set §0 is nonempty, then its image §1 is also nonempty.
This is intuitively obvious: if there is an element in §2, applying
§3 to it produces an element in §4.

In Lean, §5 means §6 -- there exists at
least one element. The proof pattern is different from the subset
and equality proofs you have been doing: instead of §7/§8,
you destructure the nonemptiness hypothesis to get a witness, then
build image membership from that witness.

**Proof plan**:
1. §9 -- extract the witness from §10
2. Construct §11 using §12 as the witness
warning: Game/Levels/ImageWorld/L18_NonemptinessTransfer.lean:28:0: No translation (en) found for: If s is nonempty, then f '' s is nonempty.
warning: Game/Levels/ImageWorld/L18_NonemptinessTransfer.lean:49:0: No translation (en) found for: You proved §0 -- image
preserves nonemptiness.

The proof was just two steps:
1. §1 -- extract the witness
2. §2 -- repackage it as image membership

**A different proof shape**: This is NOT an §3/§4 proof
like the set equality and subset proofs you have been doing. Instead,
it is a direct existential construction: get an element, transform it,
show the result is in the image.

**The converse also holds**: If §5 is nonempty, then §6 must
be nonempty. Why? Any element of §7 has a witness §8
(by definition of image membership), so §9 contains at least §10.
This means nonemptiness gives a biconditional: §11. Unlike intersection (where only ⊆ held),
nonemptiness transfers in both directions unconditionally.

**Contrast with preimage**: Preimage ALSO preserves nonemptiness
(if §12 is nonempty and intersects the range, then §13 is
nonempty), but that requires additional conditions. Image preserves
nonemptiness unconditionally -- because every element of §14 has an
output under §15.

**Boss preparation**: The boss combines image membership construction,
destructuring, range reasoning, and preimage unfolding. This level
practices the existential construction skill in a new context.

The library name is §16.

⚠ [495/631] Built Game.Levels.PsetImgPreimg.L01_ImageSubsetRange (1.3s)
warning: Game/Levels/PsetImgPreimg/L01_ImageSubsetRange.lean:6:0: No translation (en) found for: Image Subset of Range
warning: Game/Levels/PsetImgPreimg/L01_ImageSubsetRange.lean:10:0: No translation (en) found for: # Problem Set: Level 1

Welcome to the Image & Preimage Problem Set!

This warmup level asks you to prove a fundamental fact: the image of
any set is contained in the range of the function.

§0

Why? Every element of §1 has a witness §2 with §3.
That same §4 (without the source set constraint) witnesses
§5. The range is a bigger net — it captures ALL
outputs, not just those from §6.

This is a subset proof: take §7, show §8.
warning: Game/Levels/PsetImgPreimg/L01_ImageSubsetRange.lean:28:0: No translation (en) found for: The image of any set is contained in the range.
warning: Game/Levels/PsetImgPreimg/L01_ImageSubsetRange.lean:42:0: No translation (en) found for: You proved §0 — the image of any set lives inside
the range.

The proof was just two steps:
1. §1 — destructure image membership
2. §2 — reconstruct as range membership

Notice that §3 was never used! The source set membership
is irrelevant — if §4 for ANY §5, then §6.
Image membership is STRONGER than range membership because it
constrains the witness to come from §7. Dropping that constraint
makes the proof trivial.

**Image vs Range membership**:
- §8 is §9 — three components
- §10 is §11 — two components

Going from image to range means forgetting one component.

**The witness transfer pattern**: This proof illustrates a strategy
you will use throughout this problem set. Every level follows the
same two-phase structure:
1. **Destructure** one kind of membership to extract a witness
2. **Reconstruct** a different kind of membership using the same
   witness (possibly with extra work)

Recognizing this pattern — destructure, then reconstruct with the
same witness — will help you see the proof path immediately in
every level that follows.

The library name is §12.

## The Central Asymmetry

| Property | Preimage | Image |
|---|---|---|

| Preserves §32 | ✓ (equality) | No |

The structural reason: preimage involves only universal quantification
and logical connectives, which commute with everything. Image involves
an existential, which only commutes with disjunction -- not with
conjunction or negation.

principle.

## The Two Gap Closures

You have now seen both gap closures:

| Gap | Statement | Closes with |
|---|---|---|
| Image-preimage | §2 | Surjectivity (§3) |
| Preimage-image | §4 | Injectivity |

These are deep results. Injectivity means the function does not
collapse inputs — so pulling back and pushing forward recovers the
original set. Surjectivity means the function hits everything —
so pushing forward and pulling back recovers the original set.

**The duality**: Injectivity closes the preimage-image gap
(§5), and surjectivity closes the image-preimage
gap (§6). These are perfectly dual: one says the
function "doesn't merge," the other says the function "doesn't miss."
Together, they tell you exactly what properties of §7 control each
roundtrip.

If §8 is **bijective** (both injective and surjective), BOTH
roundtrip identities hold: §9 AND
§10. Image and preimage become true inverses on
the powerset. You will explore this fully in Bijective World.

## Complete Operations Table

| Operation | Image (no injectivity) | Image (with injectivity) | Preimage |
|---|---|---|---|
| Binary \\cap | only \\subseteq | **=** | = |
| Indexed \\bigcap | only \\subseteq | **=** | = |
| Binary \\cup | = | = | = |
| Indexed \\bigcup | = | = | = |
| Set difference \\ | only \\supseteq | **=** | = |
| Roundtrip with preimage | only \\supseteq | **=** | n/a |
| Roundtrip with image | n/a | n/a | only \\subseteq (= with surjectivity) |

The pattern: preimage preserves everything. Image preserves unions
but only \\subseteq for intersections and \\supseteq for differences
and roundtrips. Injectivity closes every gap in the image column.

The idea:

   §2 is equivalent to showing §3.
2. By the preimage-image embedding (§4),

3. Since §6 and §7, transitivity gives

**Direct proof (Level 6)**:

§2

**Why this matters**: The Galois proof generalizes. ANY pair of
operations related by a Galois connection (adjunction) automatically
inherits monotonicity. This principle appears throughout mathematics:
- Image/preimage of sets
- Direct image/inverse image of sheaves
- Left/right adjoints in category theory

The Galois connection is the engine; monotonicity is a free corollary.

equivalent to §34. One witness suffices
either way. For intersection, the existential does NOT distribute:
§35 implies §36, but NOT
the reverse -- the two existential witnesses might be different
elements. This is exactly the gap you just proved.

Every row says the same thing: **preimage is a Boolean algebra
homomorphism**. It maps the Boolean algebra of subsets of §39
(with §40, §41, §42, §43, §44) into the Boolean algebra
of subsets of §45, preserving all operations. It is also

composition (L14).

The single structural reason: preimage is just composition on the
predicate level. §46 is §47. Composing with
§48 does not change the logical connectives inside §49.

**The topology connection**: In topology, a function §50 is defined
to be **continuous** if and only if the preimage of every open set
is open. The preservation results you proved here are exactly why
this definition works — continuous functions automatically preserve
all the set-theoretic structure that topology needs.

**Looking ahead**: In Image World, you will study §51 —
the image of a set under a function. Image involves an existential
(§52), and existentials do NOT
commute with conjunction or negation. Image only preserves union,
not intersection or complement. The contrast between the
well-behaved preimage and the less-well-behaved image is one of
the central themes of this course.

You have proved that preimage preserves intersection, union,
complement, indexed unions, indexed intersections, and more. But
WHY does preimage preserve everything?

The answer is one equation:

§0

Preimage **reverses** function composition. If you compose §1 after
§2, then the preimage of §3 is obtained by first taking the
preimage under §4, then taking the preimage under §5.

This means preimage is just **composition on the predicate level**.
Since composition preserves all logical structure, preimage preserves
all set operations. That single fact explains every result in this
world.

Notice the order reversal: §6 applies §7 first, then §8. But
the preimage pulls back through §9 first, then §10. This is
called **contravariance** — preimage reverses the direction of
composition.

**Proof plan**:
1. §11 then §12
2. Both directions: §13 — the two sides are
   definitionally equal (both reduce to §14)

In the last two levels, you used §0, which maps each
input to a unique output (it is **injective**). But preimage is
most interesting with **non-injective** functions — functions where
multiple inputs can map to the same output.

Consider the constant function §1 — it maps EVERY
natural number to 7, regardless of the input. What is the preimage
of §2 under this function?

Since every input maps to 7, and §3, EVERY natural number
is in the preimage. Both 0 and 1000 qualify — for the same reason.

**Your task**: Prove that both 0 and 1000 are in the preimage of
§4 under the constant function §5.

**Proof plan**:
1. §6 — split the §7
2. Each part: §8 then §9

Notice that the two proofs will be **identical** — the input
doesn't matter at all.
warning: Game/Levels/PreimageWorld/L03_PreimageNonInjective.lean:36:0: No translation (en) found for: Both 0 and 1000 are in the preimage because the function always outputs 7, and 7 < 10.
warning: Game/Levels/PreimageWorld/L03_PreimageNonInjective.lean:53:0: No translation (en) found for: Both proofs were identical: §0 then §1. The input
(0 or 1000) never appeared in the proof because the constant
function ignores its input entirely.

**The preimage "expands" inputs**: For the injective function
§2, each output comes from exactly one input, so the
preimage of a set "looks like" the original set. But for a
non-injective function like §3, a single output value can
come from infinitely many inputs. The preimage of any set
containing 7 is ALL of §4.

This is why preimage is fundamentally different from function
inversion. An inverse function (when it exists) maps each output
to one input. Preimage maps each output to the set of ALL inputs
that produce it — which can be empty, finite, or infinite.

**Foreshadowing**: In Injective World, you will prove that
§5 for all §6 implies §7 is injective.
The constant function shows why this can fail: §8,
so §9.

**Looking ahead in this world**: In Level 14, you will discover
the deeper reason preimage is so well-behaved: it is just function
composition on predicates. The non-injectivity you saw here — many
inputs mapping to one output — is exactly what makes image harder
than preimage. Preimage does not care about multiple preimages;
it simply composes and checks. Injectivity matters for image, not
for preimage.

warning: Game/Levels/PreimageWorld/L08_PreimageIntersection.lean:33:0: No translation (en) found for: Preimage distributes over intersection.
warning: Game/Levels/PreimageWorld/L08_PreimageIntersection.lean:60:0: No translation (en) found for: You proved §0. Both directions

**Why both directions are the same**: The conjunction §1
IS both sides. There is no rearrangement, no extra condition, no
information loss. The §2 passes through §3 unchanged.

**The definitional tautology pattern**: When both sides of a §4 are
definitionally equal, each direction reduces to §5 —
you introduce the hypothesis and hand it right back. Call this a
**definitional tautology**: the proof is trivial because the two sides
ARE the same proposition, just written differently. You saw this in the
§6 and §7 cases, and you will see it again in the complement,
composition, and identity cases. When you notice both directions are
the same proof, it means the two sides are the same proposition under
definitional unfolding. We will use the term "definitional tautology"
to refer to this pattern going forward.

Contrast this with what happens for **images** (which you will see
later): §8 is only a
subset, NOT an equality. Image involves an existential §9, and §10 does NOT distribute over §11 in general. This
asymmetry between image and preimage is a central theme of this
course.

**Concrete counterexample**: Let §12 (constant function),
§13, §14. Then §15 and §16,
so §17. But §18, so
§19. The image of the intersection is strictly
smaller than the intersection of the images. No such failure
happens for preimage — you just proved it.

The library name is §20.

| Step | Forward (hypothesis) | Backward (goal) |
|---|---|---|
| 1. Expose negation | §1 | §2 |
| 2. Convert union | §3 | §4 |
| 3. Swap quantifier | §5 | §6 |
| 4. Convert intersection | §7 | §8 |

**§9 did the heavy lifting**: The mathematical content of
De Morgan's law is that complement swaps §10 and §11 — which is
exactly what §12 automates. In Indexed Operations World, you
proved this manually by introducing variables and deriving
contradictions. Now §13 compresses that entire argument into
one tactic call.

**Summary of the problem set**:
- Level 1: Universal set complement identity
- Level 2: Concrete type exercise on Z
- Level 3: Distributivity (abstract sets, subset direction)
- Level 4: Partition identity via antisymm + by_cases
- Level 5: Complement-difference via double negation
- Level 6: Converse complement-difference (direct proof)
- Level 7: Nested difference identity via ext + by_contra
- Level 8: Nonempty product converse
- Level 9: Indexed intersection distributes into binary intersection
- Level 10: Indexed union equals range (retrieval)
- Level 11: Powerset of intersection = intersection of powersets
- Level 12: Counterexample — powerset does not distribute over union
- Level 13: Powerset union subset — the direction that holds
- Level 14: Complement of difference via by_contra + push_neg
- Level 15: Subset characterized via set difference
- Level 16: Absorption law
- Level 17: Set difference distributes over indexed union
- Level 18 (Boss): De Morgan for indexed unions via push_neg

**The meta-principle**: Every level in this problem set reduced a
set-theoretic statement to propositional logic — then you proved the
logic. This is no coincidence. In Lean, §14: a set IS
a predicate. So every set equation reduces to a logical equivalence.
If you can prove the logic, you can prove the set identity. This is
the single most important lesson of Phase 1.

**Looking ahead**: In the next worlds, you will apply these set-theoretic
skills to **functions**: preimages (§15), images (§16), and
the fundamental asymmetry between them. Preimages preserve ALL set
operations; images only preserve unions. The membership-to-logic
reduction you have mastered is exactly what those proofs require.

Prove the **absorption law** for sets:

§0

In words: taking the union of §1 with elements that are in BOTH §2
and §3 gives back exactly §4 — because every element of §5 is
already in §6.

This is one of the fundamental Boolean algebra identities, alongside
De Morgan, distributivity, and the complement laws. It connects union
and intersection in a way that nothing else in Phase 1 does.
warning: Game/Levels/PsetSets/L16_AbsorptionLaw.lean:26:0: No translation (en) found for: The absorption law: s ∪ (s ∩ t) = s.

The proof was simple but illustrates an important point: the forward
direction required only that §1 (which is always true), and
the backward direction required only that §2 (also
always true). Absorption holds because intersection makes things
*smaller* (so §3 adds nothing to §4).

**The dual absorption law** §5 also holds: intersecting
§6 with §7 keeps exactly the elements of §8, because every
element of §9 is automatically in §10. The proof uses the same
structure with swapped roles of §11 and §12.

(Level 11) because a set that is a subset of both §6 and §7 is
automatically a subset of §8. But a subset of §9 can draw
elements from BOTH §10 and §11, without being contained in either one.

**Disproof strategy recap**:
1. §12 — assume the universal statement
2. Specialize to a concrete counterexample
3. Construct a witness that belongs to one side but not the other
4. Rewrite using the assumed equality to get a contradiction

This is the same disprove-by-counterexample pattern from Subset
World, now applied to a richer mathematical statement.

The proof has a **genuinely different structure** from the union version.

**New tactic: §2**

When you cannot build the goal directly, assume its negation and derive
a contradiction:

§3

**Why is this needed here?** The forward direction gives you
§4 and asks for §5. You cannot
directly choose §6 or §7 without knowing which of §8 or

then §11 converts §12 to §13
— which contradicts §14.

2. **Forward**: §17, §18, §19 + §20, §21

warning: Game/Levels/SetOpsWorld/L14_DualDeMorgan.lean:44:0: No translation (en) found for: Dual De Morgan: the complement of an intersection is the union
of the complements.
warning: Game/Levels/SetOpsWorld/L14_DualDeMorgan.lean:104:0: No translation (en) found for: You proved the dual De Morgan law! Notice how the proof structure
differs from Level 12:

| | Union De Morgan | Intersection De Morgan |
|---|---|---|
| **Forward** | §0 + §1 (direct) | §2 + §3 (indirect) |
| **Backward** | §4 + §5 on union | §6 + §7 + §8 on union |

The forward direction required **proof by contradiction** (§9)
because the goal was a disjunction where neither side could be chosen
without more information. This is a fundamental technique:

> When you cannot build the goal directly, assume its negation and
> derive a contradiction.

The §10 + §11 combination is especially powerful: it
converts a goal you cannot prove constructively into hypotheses you
can use to find a contradiction.

**The pattern**: Conjunction/intersection results tend to be
constructive (you can build §12 by producing each piece), while
disjunction/union results tend to require classical reasoning (you
must *choose* a side, and sometimes you cannot know which is true
without indirect argument). Watch for this asymmetry in future proofs:
if your goal is a disjunction and you cannot pick a side, §13
is likely the way forward.

**What is classical reasoning?** Classical logic means we accept that
every proposition is either true or false — there is no middle ground.
Not all proof systems accept this (constructive logic does not), but
Lean does, so §14 is always available. The philosophical
difference: in constructive logic, to prove §15 you must *exhibit*
which one holds. In classical logic, you can prove §16 by showing
that assuming neither leads to contradiction.

The library name is §17.

negation of the goal as a hypothesis, and changes the goal to §1.

§2

## When to use it
When you cannot build the goal directly. After §3, you have
§4 in context and must derive §5.

§7

## Warning
§8 uses classical logic (the law of excluded middle).
Every Lean proof may use it — there is no constructivity restriction.

set-theoretic machinery. All set reasoning IS logical reasoning.

contains elements in exactly one of the two sets. With this operation,
the subsets of any type form an abelian group (with identity §15).
We will not prove this in this course, but it is a fascinating
structural fact worth knowing.

§19 would have to be in both — contradiction.

warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L14_Antisymm.lean:8:0: No translation (en) found for: # An Alternative Route to Set Equality

In Levels 10–11, you proved set equalities using §0 — reducing to
a membership biconditional and proving both directions.

There is another route: **double containment** (also called
**antisymmetry**). If §1 AND §2, then §3.

The theorem §4 captures this:

§5

If you already have two subset proofs, you can combine them with
§6 to get equality directly — no
§7 needed.

**Your task**: Given §8 and §9, prove §10.
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L14_Antisymm.lean:28:0: No translation (en) found for: Two sets that contain each other are equal.
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L14_Antisymm.lean:61:0: No translation (en) found for: §0 gives you a one-step path from two ⊆ proofs to
set equality. Compare the two routes:

| Route | When to use |
|---|---|
| §1 | When you need to prove both directions from scratch |
| §2 | When you already have both ⊆ proofs in hand |

In practice, many set equality proofs start with §3 because you
rarely have the subset proofs pre-packaged. But §4 is
valuable when subset facts are available from other lemmas or
hypotheses.

In ordinary math, this is the well-known principle: "two sets are
equal iff each is a subset of the other." Every proof of set equality
by "double inclusion" (showing ⊆ in both directions) is implicitly
using antisymmetry.

A **partial order** is a relation that satisfies three properties:
1. **Reflexive**: §5 for all §6
2. **Transitive**: §7 and §8 imply §9
3. **Antisymmetric**: §10 and §11 imply §12

You have now proved all three for §13 on §14:
- **Reflexive**: §15 (Level 3)
- **Transitive**: §16 (Level 8)
- **Antisymmetric**: §17 (this level)

Together, these make §18 a **partial order** on sets. The partial
order concept is central to mathematics — you will study it in depth
in the Orders \\& Lattices course.

These two routes — §19 and §20 — are logically *equivalent*.
You can derive §21 from §22 and §23 from §24. You
will prove this equivalence yourself in Levels 16 and 17.

a subset of the other.

## Statement
§1

## Syntax
§2

## When to use it
When you have (or can prove) both §3 and §4 and want to
conclude §5. This is the \\"double containment\\" proof strategy.

## Example

|---|---|---|

**Decomposing §4**: The §5 and §6 projections on an §7 extract
the two directions. If §8, then:
- §9 (forward, also called §10)
- §11 (backward, also called §12)

**Mnemonic**: §13 stands for *modus ponens* (the logical rule "if P→Q
and P, then Q"). §14 is *modus ponens reverse* — it applies the
backward direction. You will see §15 and §16 throughout Lean
proofs; this naming convention makes them easy to remember.

This equivalence confirms that §17 and §18 are two faces of
the same principle. Stated as a single theorem:

**For sets §19 and §20, §21 if and only if §22.**

The axiom of extensionality and the antisymmetry of §23 are logically
interchangeable — use whichever is more convenient for the problem
at hand.

This level demonstrates the key insight: **subset proofs are about
predicates**. The §2 pattern is universal, but the closing
move depends on the relationship between the predicates:

| Predicate type | Closing move |
|---|---|
| Arithmetic (§3, §4) | §5 |
| Implication from hypothesis | §6 or §7 |
| Vacuously true (§8) | §9 |
| Trivially true (§10) | §11 |

The proof pattern is always the same:
1. §12 — enter the subset proof
2. (optional) unwrap with §13/§14
3. Close with whatever fits the specific predicates

This table will keep growing as you encounter more types of predicates
throughout the course.

All the subset proofs so far have been **positive**: you showed that
one set is contained in another. But what if the containment is
**false**? How do you prove §0?

In Lean, §1 is defined as §2. So to prove §3,
you use §4 to **assume** the subset holds, getting
§5, and then derive a contradiction (§6).

To derive the contradiction, exhibit a **counterexample**: find an
element that is in §7 but not in §8. If the subset held, this
element would have to be in both — but it is not, so we get §9.

In Level 1, we noted that §10 is false
because 4 is in the first set but not the second. Now you will prove
this formally.

**Proof plan**:
1. §11 — assume the subset holds (goal becomes §12)
2. Prove §13 — show the witness is in the domain
3. Apply §14 to get §15 — use the assumed subset
4. Unwrap and derive contradiction — §16 is false
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L07_NonSubset.lean:34:0: No translation (en) found for: The set of numbers less than 5 is NOT a subset of the set of
numbers less than 3.
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L07_NonSubset.lean:65:0: No translation (en) found for: You just proved your first **negative** subset statement! The proof
structure was:

1. **Assume** the subset holds (§0)
2. **Exhibit** a counterexample element (4)
3. **Apply** the assumed subset to the counterexample (§1)
4. **Derive contradiction** from the impossible conclusion (§2)

This is a **proof by counterexample**: to disprove a universal
statement §3, you assume it holds and exhibit a specific
§4 where §5 leads to a contradiction. Here, the universal
statement is §6, and the counterexample is §7.

This is a specific form of proof by contradiction, but the key
technique — **finding a witness that breaks the claim** — deserves its
own name. You will use proof by counterexample throughout this course:
to disprove injectivity (find two inputs with the same output), to
disprove surjectivity (find an output with no input), and to disprove
set equality (find an element in one set but not the other).

Notice how Step 3 used the subset-as-function pattern from Level 6:
§8 applies the subset hypothesis §9 to the membership proof
§10. The difference is that here, the conclusion is *absurd* — which
is exactly how we know the assumption was wrong.

In ordinary math: "Suppose §11. Then in
particular §12, which is false. Contradiction."

⚠ [583/631] Built Game.Levels.SubsetWorld.L10_ExtIntro (1.7s)
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L10_ExtIntro.lean:6:0: No translation (en) found for: Set Equality with ext
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L10_ExtIntro.lean:8:0: No translation (en) found for: # Extensionality: When Two Sets Are Equal

Two sets are equal when they have exactly the same elements:

§0

This is the **Axiom of Extensionality** — one of the foundational
axioms of set theory. It says that a set is nothing more than its
elements: two sets with the same members are the same set, regardless
of how they are described. The tactic §1
converts a set equality goal into: for each §2, prove the membership
biconditional §3.

After §4, the goal becomes an §5. Use §6 to split it
into two implications (forward and backward), then prove each direction.

After §7, you will see the §8 (focus dot) for the first time.
When Lean creates multiple subgoals, §9 **focuses** on one subgoal at
a time. Write §10 to apply §11 to the current subgoal only.
After that subgoal is closed, the next §12 picks up the next subgoal.
Think of §13 as "now working on this case."

**Your task**: Prove that §14.

The conjunction §15 is equivalent to just §16 because
§17 already implies §18. So the second condition is redundant.

This level is longer than previous ones — the proof has several steps.
Do not worry: each step is guided by hints, and the math is simple.
The length comes from the proof structure, not from difficulty.

**Proof plan**:
1. §19 — reduce to membership biconditional
2. §20 — split the §21 into two directions
3. **Forward** (§22): given §23, extract §24
4. **Backward** (§25): given §26, build §27
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L10_ExtIntro.lean:47:0: No translation (en) found for: A conjunction with a weaker condition is equivalent to the
stronger condition alone.
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L10_ExtIntro.lean:101:0: No translation (en) found for: You proved your first set equality using §0! The structure was:

§1

**Why §2 works**: Since §3, two sets are equal when
they are equal as functions. The §4 tactic applies **function
extensionality**: two functions are equal when they agree on all inputs.
For sets, "agreeing on all inputs" means having the same membership
predicate — which is exactly §5. So set
extensionality is not an extra axiom — it IS function extensionality
applied to predicates.

In ordinary math, this proof would read: "We show the sets are equal
by double inclusion. If §6 and §7, then certainly §8.
Conversely, if §9, then also §10 (since §11), so
§12."

**The §13 (focus dot)**: After §14 splits a goal into two
subgoals, §15 focuses on one at a time. This keeps your proof
organized — you handle one case completely before moving to the next.
You will use §16 in nearly every proof that involves §17.

**Dot projections on §18**: If §19, then §20 and
§21. This is the quickest way to extract one component of a
conjunction. The same §22/§23 pattern works on §24 (biconditionals)
too — you will use it later to extract the forward and backward
implications.

**Destructuring §25**: When a hypothesis is a conjunction §26,
you can write §27 to introduce and split it in one step,
instead of §28 followed by §29. This
shorthand will be especially useful in the next world when working
with intersections (§30), which are defined as conjunctions.

**Why §31 works on §32**: The §33 type has exactly one
constructor, §34, which takes two implications as arguments.
When you write §35 on a §36 goal, Lean applies §37
and asks you for the two implications — just as §38 on §39
applies §40. This is why the same tactic works for both
conjunctions and biconditionals.

The §41 tactic is the primary tool for set equality proofs in Lean.
You will use it frequently throughout this course.

warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L18_Boss.lean:8:0: No translation (en) found for: # Boss: Chaining Subsets to Prove Equality

You have two sets §0 and §1 of natural numbers, with three
hypotheses:
- §2 — §3 is contained in §4
- §5 — §6 is contained in the numbers at most 4
- §7 — every number less than 5 is in §8

Your task: prove §9.

Think about what this means. The hypothesis §10 tells you that
§11. And from §12 and §13, you can chain:
§14. Since §15 is the same as §16 for
natural numbers, this gives §17. Together with
§18, you get equality by antisymmetry.

This problem integrates the core skills of this world:
- **§19 or §20** to prove equality
- **§21** to prove subsets
- **§22/§23** to unwrap set membership
- **Chaining subset hypotheses** (transitivity from Level 8)
- **§24** to bridge arithmetic facts

There are multiple valid strategies. Choose your own path!
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L18_Boss.lean:35:0: No translation (en) found for: If s ⊆ t ⊆ {n | n ≤ 4} and every n < 5 is in s, then s = {n | n < 5}.
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L18_Boss.lean:127:0: No translation (en) found for: Congratulations — you have completed **Subset World**!

Here is your toolkit:

| Concept | Lean | Proof move |
|---|---|---|
| Subset §0 | §1 | §2 then show §3 |
| Reflexivity §4 | identity | §5 |
| §6 | vacuously true | §7 |
| §8 | trivially true | §9 |
| Transitivity | §10 | chain with §11 or function application |
| Set equality via ext | §12 | §13 |
| Set equality via antisymm | §14 | §15 |
| Set non-equality §16 | §17 | assume equality, find contradictory witness |
| Proper subset §18 | §19 | §20, then prove ⊆ and ¬⊆ |
| Unwrap hypothesis | §21 | converts display to def-equal form |
| Dot projection | §22, §23 | extract components of §24 or §25 |

The §26 relation is a **partial order** on sets: it is reflexive
(Level 3), transitive (Level 8), and antisymmetric (Level 14). The
strict variant §27 (proper subset, Level 15) excludes equality.

In the next world, you will learn about **set operations**: union (§28),
intersection (§29), complement (§30), and difference (§31intro x hx` proof
pattern you mastered here will be the foundation for every one of
those proofs.

So far, you have been **proving** subset relations from scratch using
§0. But what about **using** a subset hypothesis that you
already have?

If §1, then §2 is essentially a **function**: give it a
proof that §3, and it returns a proof that §4. This is
because §5 unfolds to §6, which is exactly
the type of a function from membership proofs to membership proofs.

In this level, you have §7 and §8. Your goal is
§9. Since §10 is a function that takes §11 to §12,
you can close the goal with §13.

Alternatively, you can use §14 to reduce the goal from §15
to §16, then close with §17.

**Proof plan**:
- §18 — apply §19 to §20 directly
- OR: §21 then §22 — reduce goal, then close
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L06_SubsetHypAsFunction.lean:32:0: No translation (en) found for: If s ⊆ t and x ∈ s, then x ∈ t.
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld/L06_SubsetHypAsFunction.lean:49:0: No translation (en) found for: You just used a subset hypothesis as a function for the first time!

The key insight: §0 is not just a statement — it is a
**function** that converts membership proofs. Given §1,
the expression §2 is a proof of §3.

| Expression | Type | Meaning |
|---|---|---|
| §4 | §5 | "every element of s is in t" |
| §6 | §7 | "x is in s" |
| §8 | §9 | "therefore x is in t" |

**Under the hood**: §10 has the type §11 —
which IS a function type. When you write §12, you are doing
ordinary function application: feeding a membership proof to a
function that converts membership proofs. This is not a special rule
for subsets — it is the same Curry-Howard correspondence you saw in
Set World, where membership itself is function application.

This pattern — applying a subset hypothesis to a membership proof —
is the fundamental way to *use* subset facts. You will chain multiple
such applications in the next levels.

In Lean 4, a **set** is not a primitive concept — it is built from
something you already know: **predicates**.

The definition is:
§0

A set of natural numbers is just a function that takes a natural number
and returns a proposition. If the proposition is true, the number is
*in* the set; if false, it is *not* in the set.

**Membership** §1 means: apply the predicate §2 to §3 and check
whether the result holds. So §4 is just §5 — function application!

The simplest set is §6, the **universal set**. Its predicate is
§7 — it returns §8 for every input. So §9
reduces to just §10.

the goal state shows §12 — because Lean has already evaluated the
membership predicate for you. All you need to do is prove §13.

is a type with exactly one constructor, §16, and §17
applies it.)

Given a function §0 and a set §1 (a set of outputs),
the **preimage** of §2 under §3 is the set of all inputs whose output
lands in §4:

§5

In Lean, this is written §6 (type §7).

Preimage is defined by a single, beautiful principle:
**membership in a preimage is function application followed by a
membership check.** Nothing more. There is no existential, no witness,
no construction — just "apply §8, then check if the result is in §9."

This simplicity has a remarkable consequence: **preimage preserves
every set operation**. Intersection, union, complement, difference,
indexed unions, indexed intersections — they all distribute over
preimage. The logical connectives (§10, §11, §12, §13, §14) pass
through function application unchanged.

By the end of this world, you will:
- Prove concrete preimage membership by computing §15
- See why non-injective functions make preimage interesting
- Learn the **fiber** vocabulary (preimage of a singleton)
- Prove preimage is monotone (bigger target → bigger preimage)
- Prove preimage preserves §16, §17, §18, §19, §20, §21, §22, and §23rw§24f ⁻¹' t§25\\-1'§26t§27f`.

You have mastered preimages -- pulling sets back through functions. Now you
will study the dual operation: **images** -- pushing sets forward.

Given a function §0 and a set §1 (a set of inputs),
the **image** of §2 under §3 is the set of all outputs that come from
inputs in §4:

§5

In Lean, this is written §6 (type §7).

**The critical difference from preimage**: Image membership involves an
**existential**. To prove §8, you must **exhibit a witness** --
an input §9 such that §10. Preimage membership was just
function application (§11 -- no witness needed).
Image membership requires a construction.

This existential has far-reaching consequences:
- Image preserves union: **yes** (equality)
- Image preserves intersection: **no** (only ⊆, not =)
- Image preserves complement: **no**

Compare with preimage, which preserves ALL set operations. The
difference comes down to logic: preimage uses §12, §13, §14 which
commute with function application. Image uses §15, which does NOT
commute with §16 or §17.

By the end of this world, you will:
- Prove image membership by exhibiting witnesses
- Learn §18 and §19 -- power tools for pattern matching
- Prove §20 -- the vacuous extremal case
- Prove §21 -- the simplest image
- Prove image preserves union but NOT intersection
- See a concrete counterexample for intersection equality
- Prove image distributes over function composition
- Learn §22 -- the image of everything
- Prove that §23
- Prove the Galois connection: §24
- Explore the image-preimage gaps and when they close
- Re-derive monotonicity AND the image-preimage gap from the Galois connection
- Prove image preserves nonemptiness (both directions)

The boss asks you to prove that §25 when every element
of §26 is in the range of §27 -- the image-preimage gap closes with a
surjectivity condition.

**New notation**: §28 (typed §29) -- the image of §30 under §31.

a §7 value from a natural number §8 known to satisfy §9,
write §10. For example, §11 creates a valid

1. §14 — reduce to membership biconditional

warning: Game/Levels/IndexedOpsWorld/L17_IUnionEqualsUniv.lean:39:0: No translation (en) found for: The remainder classes mod 3 cover all natural numbers.
warning: Game/Levels/IndexedOpsWorld/L17_IUnionEqualsUniv.lean:75:0: No translation (en) found for: You proved that the remainder classes mod 3 cover everything. The proof
combined §0 (from Subset World) with §1 (from this world):

§2

**The reusable recipe**: To prove §3, use §4
to reduce to membership, then for the backward direction, provide a
witness index that covers the given element. The forward direction
is always trivial (everything is in §5).

**Mathematical content**: The remainder classes §6
for §7 form a **partition** of §8. A partition
has two properties:

1. **Covers everything**: §9 — you just proved this.
2. **Pairwise disjoint**: §10 for §11 — you did NOT prove this.

You showed the union covers everything, but a partition also requires
that the pieces don't overlap. You will prove disjointness properties
later when we study partitions and equivalence relations.

⚠ [616/631] Built Game.Levels.IndexedOpsWorld.L09_VacuousIntersection (1.4s)
warning: Game/Levels/IndexedOpsWorld/L09_VacuousIntersection.lean:6:0: No translation (en) found for: Vacuous Intersection
warning: Game/Levels/IndexedOpsWorld/L09_VacuousIntersection.lean:8:0: No translation (en) found for: # When the Index Type Is Empty

What happens when you take the intersection over an *empty* family of
sets? If the index type has no elements at all, then §0 should
contain everything — because the condition §1 is
**vacuously true** when there are no indices to check.

This is one of the most surprising properties of indexed intersections.
When you intersect over nothing, you get everything:

§2

Students often picture intersections as always "shrinking" a collection.
But when there are no constraints, there is nothing to shrink — every
element satisfies all zero conditions.

This is also why **bounded** variants (§3) exist: they let
you restrict to a non-vacuous index set, avoiding this surprise.

**Your task**: Prove that the intersection over the empty index type
is §4.
warning: Game/Levels/IndexedOpsWorld/L09_VacuousIntersection.lean:36:0: No translation (en) found for: The intersection over an empty index type is the universal set.
warning: Game/Levels/IndexedOpsWorld/L09_VacuousIntersection.lean:67:0: No translation (en) found for: You proved that §0 — intersecting over
an empty index gives everything.

The key insight is **vacuous truth**: the statement §1
is true because there are no §2 to check. In Lean, §3 is a type
with no constructors, so §4 (equivalently, §5)
discharges any goal about an element of §6.

**§7 and §8**: The pattern §9 for §10
is the type-level version of §11 for §12. Both say
"from an impossible premise, anything follows" — the principle of
explosion. You will see §13 in propositional logic and
§14 when working with uninhabited types (like §15).

**Why this is the right convention**: The intersection of a collection of
sets is the **largest set contained in all of them**. When the collection
is empty, every set is vacuously contained in all (zero) members of the
collection — so the largest such set is §16. This is the same
pattern as:
- An empty product equals 1 (the multiplicative identity)
- An empty sum equals 0 (the additive identity)
- An empty conjunction (∀ over nothing) is §17

In each case, the empty operation returns the **identity element** for
that operation. For intersection, the identity is §18 (since
§19 for all §20). For union, the identity is §21
(since §22 for all §23). This is why the dual result —
§24 — is also true.

**Why this matters**: This explains why **bounded** indexed intersections
(§25) are needed. If §26 might be empty, the bounded
intersection degenerates to §27, which is often not what you
want. When you see a bounded intersection in a proof, always consider:
what happens if the bounding set is empty?

**Up next**: You will prove the dual — §28 —
completing the most dramatic duality pair in this world.

In ordinary math: "The intersection §29
contains every element, because the defining condition — §30
for all §31 — is vacuously satisfied."

§7

The binary union §8 is the special case with a two-element index
(§9). The indexed version generalizes §10 to §11:

| Operation | Notation | Logical meaning |
|---|---|---|
| Binary union | §12 | §13 |
| Indexed union | §14 | §15 |

⚠ [625/631] Built Game.Levels.SubsetWorld (1.0s)
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld.lean:21:0: No translation (en) found for: Subset World
warning: Game/Levels/SubsetWorld.lean:23:0: No translation (en) found for: # Subset World

In Set World, you learned that sets are predicates and membership is
predicate evaluation. Now you will learn the two fundamental
relationships between sets: **subset** (§0) and **equality** (§1).

A **subset** proof §2 means: for every element, if it is in §3
then it is in §4. The proof shape is always §5 — fix an
element, assume it is in the smaller set, then show it is in the
larger set.

A **set equality** proof §6 means: the two sets have exactly the
same elements. There are two routes:
- **Extensionality** (§7): prove §8 directly
- **Double containment** (§9): prove §10 and
  §11 separately

By the end of this world, you will:
- Prove subset relations with the §12 pattern
- Handle the boundary cases §13 and §14
- Use subset hypotheses as functions
- Disprove false subset claims by contradiction
- Chain subset proofs transitively
- Prove set equality via §15 (extensionality)
- Prove set equality via §16 (double containment)
- Prove set non-equality by finding a contradictory witness
- Prove proper subset (§17) by combining §18 and §19
- Use §20 to unwrap set notation on hypotheses

You will also gain a new tactic (§21) and a new proof strategy
(double containment) that you will use throughout the rest of this
course.

**New tools in this world**:
- §22 — unwrap a hypothesis to a definitionally equal form
- §23 — reduce set equality to element-wise membership
- §24 — derive equality from mutual subsets

warning: Game/Levels/SetOpsWorld.lean:22:0: No translation (en) found for: # Set Operations World

In Set World, you learned that sets are predicates. In Subset World,
you learned to prove subset and equality relationships between sets.
Now you will learn the four fundamental **set operations** — and
discover that each one is a logical connective in disguise:

| Operation | Notation | Logical meaning |
|---|---|---|
| Union | §0 | §1 |
| Intersection | §2 | §3 |
| Complement | §4 | §5 |
| Difference | §6 | §7 |

This correspondence is the deepest fact about sets in Lean: **every
set operation reduces to a propositional connective**. Set-theoretic
reasoning IS logical reasoning.

By the end of this world, you will be able to:
- Prove membership in unions, intersections, complements, and differences
- Prove subset relations involving these operations
- Perform case analysis on union hypotheses
- Prove full set equalities like §8 using §9
- Use §10 to simplify negated compound propositions
- Prove De Morgan's laws for sets (both directions)
- Use §11 for proof by contradiction
- Prove the complement laws, double complement, and distributivity

**New tools in this world**:
- §12 / §13 — choose a side of a disjunction (or union)
- §14 — push negation through logical connectives
- §15 — proof by contradiction
- §16 — case analysis on disjunctions
  (the §17 tactic is familiar; what is new here are the constructor
  names §18 and §19 for the two branches of §20)

**Prerequisites**: Set World (membership, show/change) and Subset World
(intro x hx, ext, constructor, obtain).

warning: Game/Levels/SetWorld.lean:14:0: No translation (en) found for: # Set World

Welcome to the first world of **Functions & Relations**!

In Lean 4, a set is not a primitive concept — it is built from
predicates. The definition is strikingly simple:

§0

A set of natural numbers is just a function from natural numbers to
propositions. An element is *in* the set when the proposition it maps
to is true.

This means all set-theoretic reasoning reduces to propositional logic.
Membership is function application. The empty set uses §1. The
universal set uses §2. In this world, you will internalize this
reduction by working through concrete examples.

By the end of this world, you will be able to:
- Prove membership in sets defined by predicates
- Prove non-membership using negation
- Destructure existential witnesses with §3
- Work with §4 and §5
- Handle compound predicates with logical connectives
- Recognize that set membership IS predicate evaluation

**Prerequisites**: You should be comfortable with §6, §7,
§8, §9, and §10 from the Natural Number Game.

The boss at the end will ask you to combine all these moves on an
abstract predicate. Let's begin!

**The extract-transform-repack pattern**: This proof follows a
three-step strategy that recurs throughout indexed operations:

1. **Extract** — unpack the hypothesis to get a witness or universal
2. **Transform** — apply a given relationship (here, §1)
3. **Repack** — rebuild the goal using the transformed data

We will call this the *extract-transform-repack* pattern. You will
see it again in monotonicity of intersection (next level), De Morgan
laws, and the boss level.

In ordinary math: "If §2, then §3 for
some §4. Since §5, we have §6. Therefore
§7."
